<div id="review_295598201" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/295598201" />
    <a title="Diane" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/279256-diane"><img alt="Diane" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1497365060p2/279256.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/295598201?book_show_action=true">Jan 23, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Diane" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Diane" href="/user/show/279256-diane">Diane</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141441283" href="/book/show/1294819.The_Wings_of_the_Dove">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/279256-diane?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/279256-diane?shelf=audiobooks">audiobooks</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer295598201" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17607872115414100461">I didn’t love this novel as I had hoped. So many others have praised Henry James that I expected to be overjoyed while reading “The Wings of the Dove,” but that didn’t happen. <br/><br/>I liked it enough, but I wasn’t blown away by the prose or breathlessly underlining quotes as I had anticipated. The story is one of greed, jealousy, heartbreak and grief. There is also love, but it is corrupted. <br/><br/>Maybe I didn’t like this novel much because the female protagonist, Kate Croy, is painted as cruel and calculat</span>
  <span id="freeText17607872115414100461" style="display:none">I didn’t love this novel as I had hoped. So many others have praised Henry James that I expected to be overjoyed while reading “The Wings of the Dove,” but that didn’t happen. <br /><br />I liked it enough, but I wasn’t blown away by the prose or breathlessly underlining quotes as I had anticipated. The story is one of greed, jealousy, heartbreak and grief. There is also love, but it is corrupted. <br /><br />Maybe I didn’t like this novel much because the female protagonist, Kate Croy, is painted as cruel and calculating by James. The dying girl, Milly, is frequently described as being magnificent and spectacular, but she shows no outstanding qualities of character other than being wealthy, young and that she’ll be dead soon. And then there’s Merton, the poor man caught in the love triangle and manipulated by Kate.<br /><br />After finishing the novel I watched the 1997 film version, and I have to admit I liked the movie better than the book. Mostly because the Kate character in the movie is at least shown with a broken heart and being genuinely conflicted. The screenplay made Kate more likeable.<br /><br />I haven’t completely given up on Henry James. I still need to read “The Portrait of a Lady.” Maybe I’ll like that one more.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17607872115414100461" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_463465653" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/463465653" />
    <a title="David" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11003362-david"><img alt="David" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1342451018p2/11003362.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/463465653?book_show_action=true">Nov 25, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David" href="/user/show/11003362-david">David</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780679600671" href="/book/show/1571114.The_Wings_of_the_Dove">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11003362-david?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer463465653" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13223748017130925717">The Wings of the Dove			Henry James (1902) #26<br/><br/>June 8, 2007<br/><br/>	This has to be the worst book that I have ever read. Well, maybe not ever, but definitely the worst one yet on this list (and there have been some that have sucked mightily). How this book made the list, I have no idea, but it has rattled my already shaky faith in the validity of this list. I suspect that it has something to do with one Mr. Gore Vidal, who, judging from the little blurb on the back of the dust jacket of the copy that I h</span>
  <span id="freeText13223748017130925717" style="display:none">The Wings of the Dove			Henry James (1902) #26<br /><br />June 8, 2007<br /><br />	This has to be the worst book that I have ever read. Well, maybe not ever, but definitely the worst one yet on this list (and there have been some that have sucked mightily). How this book made the list, I have no idea, but it has rattled my already shaky faith in the validity of this list. I suspect that it has something to do with one Mr. Gore Vidal, who, judging from the little blurb on the back of the dust jacket of the copy that I have, thinks that Henry James is the be-all to end-all of modern literature. If I ever see Mr. Vidal (and I don’t even know if he is even still with us), I will be bound by honor to kick his ass for making me read this book. There are two more books of Mr. James’ on this list, and I hope that they are significantly shorter than this one. I own a handsome copy of The Ambassadors, and was quite proud of obtaining it before reading this book. Now I think that I might use it to soak up some oil stains in my driveway. At least it’s only just over 300 pages long. <br />	To the book – James spends a huge amount of time being so descriptive about pointless things that the reader is left without a clue as to what is happening in the book, or who anyone is, or what he or she is even dong there. When I first discovered that I didn’t know what was happening in the story (and that I couldn’t relate with any of the characters), I thought that maybe I was too unlearned to understand James’ writing. After 40 or 50 more pages, I realized that the problem didn’t lie with me (or my lack of understanding, cognition, intelligence, ect..), it was just that the writing sucked. And sucked it did. For 700+ pages that dragged on and on.<br />	I am happy to report that I only paid a dollar for my copy of this book, which was withdrawn from the Conroe, Texas library, possibly in fear that it might, by some strange literary osmosis, make all the other books on the shelf suck as well. It had only been read once, by some unfortunate soul who was a sloppy reader and had spilled soup or something on the pages and had only made it to page 100 or so before either personally returning it to the library, or having it returned to the library by some friend or family member after prying it from the dead, cold hands of a reader that had eaten poison to relieve the painful existence that their life had become after reading part of this book. I digress…<br />	It only took 540 pages of agony for this book to get in the least bit interesting. Kate Croy appeals to Merton Densher (yes, Merton), her lover (of a Nineteenth Century sort), to marry her dying rich friend Milly so that when Milly dies, Merton will inherit her money and can then be “eligible” to marry Kate. Densher acquiesces to Kate’s proposal only if she will sleep with him that night, which she apparently does.<br />	There was a time in this novel, around page 585, which I briefly took interest in both the books plot and it’s characters, but I quickly lost interest again. I had, after all, just figured out that Mrs. Stringham was Susan Shepherd, and that led me to assume that Mrs. Lowder, i.e. Aunt Maud, was Maud Maningham, a fact that should have been made clear at the onset of the book. Suspense in writing should not come from figuring out to whom the author is referring to unless they are some kind of spy, double agent, or in some alternate reality. Thanks for the clarity, Henry.<br />	Looking back, I should have known that a book whose main character (or one of them) is named “Merton” would spell trouble. What the hell kind of name is Merton, anyway? Austrian? Dutch? I do know that Merton Densher comes close to sharing with Dick Diver the most unfortunate name in literature. Come to think of it, I want my dollar back.<br /><br />0.7<br /><br />A (although referred to off-handedly), T, N<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13223748017130925717" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_42149367" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/42149367" />
    <a title="David" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1493234-david"><img alt="David" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1255663765p2/1493234.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/42149367?book_show_action=true">Jan 06, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David" href="/user/show/1493234-david">David</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer42149367" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7426050453492965666">Gives new meaning to the expression "adventures in reading." The famous style of the late novels--the monster sentences with multiple subordinate elements that snake their way through paragraphs extending for page after page after page--will try your patience and reward your attentions--and yet frequently frustrate your best attempts at comprehension. I found the experience altogether fascinating, both in its challenges and its rewards--and there were wonderful rewards, passages and situations t</span>
  <span id="freeText7426050453492965666" style="display:none">Gives new meaning to the expression "adventures in reading." The famous style of the late novels--the monster sentences with multiple subordinate elements that snake their way through paragraphs extending for page after page after page--will try your patience and reward your attentions--and yet frequently frustrate your best attempts at comprehension. I found the experience altogether fascinating, both in its challenges and its rewards--and there were wonderful rewards, passages and situations that moved me to tears and to laughter both. The problem of the style remains just that--a problem, and one that reading should not try to make disappear too quickly, whether in the negative mode of curt dismissal (which renders curiosity about the style pointless) or the positive mode of effusive admiration (which renders curiosity needless). (Cf. reviews other readers have posted on Goodreads.)<br /><br /><br />I found it interesting to observe the ghost of Hawthorne in the way James manages his narrative. As in The Scarlet Letter, the surfaces of conversation and social action are mere occasions that serve to confirm the slow deep movement of motive and feeling that James is at such pains to render in that so frequently maddening prose. There are episodes between Kate Croy and Densher that in many ways recreate those between Hester Prynne and Dimmesdale; and Jane Austen reappears in certain of James' situations and plot devices--the secret engagement between Kate Croy and Merton Densher replays, and in interesting ways revises, the secret engagement of Frank Churchill and Jane Fairfax in Austen's Emma. (The impecunious Kate Croy is as emphatically active and morally implicated as the equally impecunious Jane Fairfax is passive and morally blameless.)<br /><br /><br />I was struck how, like his brother William, Henry James is at bottom a depth psychologist of extraordinary orginality and penetration. Is part of the trouble this book gives us that so much of what it psychologically finds as it investigates the case of Kate Croy, Merton Densher, and Milly Theale does not conform to our assumptions, expectations, and understandings about how characters are supposed to think and feel and act, in novels or in life? At the end, there remain passages that elude me--James' perceptions remain beyond me at many points. Yet the book offers a multitude of satisfactions--it is, after all, a first-rate romantic thriller, full of suspenseful episodes and populated with a cast of fascinating characters whose intrigues are fascinatingly presented and developed. As so often in literature, the greatness of this work resides as much or more in its difficulties and strangenesses as in the sweets--and they are many--it serves up for our taste.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7426050453492965666" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer4633632929595171808">I have a thing for Henry James. When I read The Golden Bowl in grad school and loved it, my professor thought I was crazy. I suppose many students find him dense and confusing. But I love his style. I love the careful, layered characterizations, the detailed descriptions, and the way James' novels flow mostly from internal dialogue. His artistry is in telling the story through the consciousness of the characters. I simply love it!<br/><br/>p.s. I find it funny {and revealing} that his novels translated to</span>
  <span id="freeText4633632929595171808" style="display:none">I have a thing for Henry James. When I read The Golden Bowl in grad school and loved it, my professor thought I was crazy. I suppose many students find him dense and confusing. But I love his style. I love the careful, layered characterizations, the detailed descriptions, and the way James' novels flow mostly from internal dialogue. His artistry is in telling the story through the consciousness of the characters. I simply love it!<br /><br />p.s. I find it funny {and revealing} that his novels translated to the movie screen are R rated. Or very sensual. Gotta love the undercurrent of Victorian sensuality--in the books the untold is the told.</span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audiobook " href="/book/show/35076631-the-wings-of-the-dove">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2352900-elaine?shelf=2017">2017</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2352900-elaine?shelf=audio">audio</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1992658659" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9547661506869896195">This is probably not the best choice for an audio book - it is really quite intricate, and I struggled at times to find the referent in some of these Jamesian sentences, even as Juliet Stevenson did her usual remarkable job. <br/><br/>As with Portrait of the Lady, I managed to forget the exact contours of the plot since my first reading some 25 years ago or so. And as with Portrait of the Lady, I spent the early part of the book not really loving it. I found too little to get a grip on in the beginning. B</span>
  <span id="freeText9547661506869896195" style="display:none">This is probably not the best choice for an audio book - it is really quite intricate, and I struggled at times to find the referent in some of these Jamesian sentences, even as Juliet Stevenson did her usual remarkable job. <br /><br />As with Portrait of the Lady, I managed to forget the exact contours of the plot since my first reading some 25 years ago or so. And as with Portrait of the Lady, I spent the early part of the book not really loving it. I found too little to get a grip on in the beginning. But then, oh, of course, James' grand design starts to pay off, and the elaborate gradations of sentiment and nuance become rather heartpoundingly immediate and intense. It is not my first thought when I think of James, but I literally could not stop listening by the end. What can I say? The Master is The Master is The Master. I bow down. <br /><br />I watched the movie concurrently with listening to the end of the book. The movie is luscious, but has little to do with the book except in the roughest outlines and everyone becomes much cruder (of necessity, I guess). So both are enjoyable, but two different enjoyments entirely.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9547661506869896195" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780192816313" href="/book/show/3719259-the-wings-of-the-dove">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/135573-rebecca?shelf=faves">faves</a>,

          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2047001" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer18157496975561528240">Amazing book. I kept thinking, Why can't he just come out and tell us straight what he is talking about? Then I realized that James is reflecting his characters, who live in an underground world of plunder and plot, innuendo and insinuation. The last few chapters suddenly break into lucid writing, a sign that at least one character is starting to think and act straight. I need to read this book again. This book needs to be experienced.</span>

<div id="review_215901703" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/215901703" />
    <a title="Gary Inbinder" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5963850-gary-inbinder"><img alt="Gary Inbinder" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442496373p2/5963850.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/215901703?book_show_action=true">Sep 27, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gary Inbinder" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gary Inbinder" href="/user/show/5963850-gary-inbinder">Gary Inbinder</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5963850-gary-inbinder?shelf=british-literature">british-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5963850-gary-inbinder?shelf=victorian-literature">victorian-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer215901703&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer215901703" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17577134626257446626">(Second reading. Originally read in 2011)<br/><br/><br/>Henry James’s late period masterpiece is a novel about conflicting human needs and desires, for love, both physical and spiritual, for financial security, and for fulfillment and self-respect. Kate Croy, the active protagonist in this drama, begins the story in a state of existential conflict from which she struggles and schemes to extricate herself as the intricate, character-driven plot unfolds.<br/>Kate is a penniless young woman dependent upon the good wil</span>
  <span id="freeText17577134626257446626" style="display:none">(Second reading. Originally read in 2011)<br /><br /><br />Henry James’s late period masterpiece is a novel about conflicting human needs and desires, for love, both physical and spiritual, for financial security, and for fulfillment and self-respect. Kate Croy, the active protagonist in this drama, begins the story in a state of existential conflict from which she struggles and schemes to extricate herself as the intricate, character-driven plot unfolds.<br />Kate is a penniless young woman dependent upon the good will of her wealthy, social-climbing aunt. Kate is attractive and fashionable; her aunt has brought Kate out in London society with the goal of arranging an advantageous marriage for her niece. Moreover, Kate carries the burden of a needy, good-for-nothing father and a widowed sister living in borderline poverty with her children. <br />If Kate marries according to her aunt’s wishes, she and her family will be financially secure. However, Kate is in love with Merton Densher, a handsome, charming but poor journalist. What to do? Opportunity knocks in the form of Millie Theale, an immensely wealthy young American woman with no family of her own. And Millie has a secret: she’s dying of a mysterious disease. When Kate learns the secret, she devises a scheme that might solve all her problems. But then, to quote Robert Burns: “The best laid schemes o’ mice and men gang aft a-gley; An’ lea’e us nought but grief and pain, for promised joy.”<br />Did James write an allegory? The title might refer to the familiar Christian iconographic symbol for the Holy Spirit, and Millie’s gift of her entire fortune to Merton, which comes at Christmas, might be interpreted as Divine Grace. In this allegorical context, Kate and Merton stand-in for a sinful Adam and Eve and the various relationships among the characters, the situations and settings can support such an interpretation. For example, the avaricious Lord Mark who pursues Kate for sex and gain, and when that doesn’t work out switches his attention to the dying Millie, makes a good Satan. However, most critics I’ve read have either avoided or rejected this interpretation for a variety of reasons. Nevertheless, the allegory makes sense of the enigmatic ending.<br />Merton wants to marry Kate, without Millie’s money. Kate agrees, but only on condition that Merton give her his word of honor that he’s not in love with Millie’s memory. She adds this emphasis: “Her memory’s your love. You want no other.”<br />Instead of answering Kate, he says he’ll marry her “As we were.” However, the novel ends with Kate’s final words: “We shall never be again as we were!”<br />If you buy into the allegory, Kate/Eve has told Adam/Merton that he loves the memory of a dead God more than he loves her. He replies that he’ll marry her “as we were,” by implication before the fall. Kate/Eve has the last word: we can never be again as we were, in Eden. Without the allegory, you have a conflict between Kate’s self-regard and need for Merton’s unconditional love up against Merton’s bad conscience for playing his part in Kate’s scheme.<br />Should this novel be read as an allegory? Not necessarily, but I think it’s worth mentioning only because James insinuated it into his text. James was interested in the use of symbolism and allegory in art. And H.J. was certainly interested in his brother William’s investigation of religion from a psychological, sociological and philosophical standpoint: See William James’s The Will to Believe and The Varieties of Religious Experience.<br />The novel is a fine example of James’s late period style, especially his usage of a stream of consciousness. He had his own peculiar way of drawing the reader into a character’s head through a sequence of sense impressions, associations, actions and reactions, a subtle juxtaposition of epistemology and the art of narrative. That’s the upside. The downside is convoluted sentences that run as long as paragraphs and paragraphs that run more than a page. Nevertheless, if you have the time and patience, this novel’s definitely worth the effort.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17577134626257446626" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/37374526-classic-reverie?shelf=american-writer">american-writer</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/37374526-classic-reverie?shelf=1900">1900</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/37374526-classic-reverie?shelf=romance">romance</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/37374526-classic-reverie?shelf=henry-james">henry-james</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer1570419660&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1570419660" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer706224708014069897">I had seen The Heiress with Olivia de Havilland years ago on TCM & loved it, Olivia was superb in this. Then years later I found out that it was based on Henry James' Washington Square which I then read & loved. So James was on my list of authors to read again & not reading this edition but a Delphi Kindle collection of his works, I choose at random "The Wings of the Dove". Interesting in this collection they mentioned two movies based on the novel, one was a French version & 1997 Indie version </span>
  <span id="freeText706224708014069897" style="display:none">I had seen The Heiress with Olivia de Havilland years ago on TCM &amp; loved it, Olivia was superb in this. Then years later I found out that it was based on Henry James' Washington Square which I then read &amp; loved. So James was on my list of authors to read again &amp; not reading this edition but a Delphi Kindle collection of his works, I choose at random "The Wings of the Dove". Interesting in this collection they mentioned two movies based on the novel, one was a French version &amp; 1997 Indie version with Helena Bonham Carter. I liked her in many films in the past &amp; it would be interesting seeing her as Kate Croy and if I run across it I might watch it. Henry James was an American born writer whose travels landed him in England were he lived. During World War 1 prior to America's involvement James renounced his American citizenship due to US neutrality policy from involvement in that war. I found that interesting because of the unnecessary loss of lives during that war but it is always easier to see the past with the eyes of future &amp; harder to judge during those times. This book has nothing to do with the war but this author brings a special kind of perspective of being an American &amp; British citizen which shows in his works. This was published in 1902. Now to my thoughts on "The Wings of the Dove" which is a book I had a tough time understanding at times because of the flow being hard due to his sentence construction &amp; many thoughts into one sentence which made me have to read some multiple times to get the full meaning &amp; numerous innuendoes. The way he had his characters in passages makes it confusing sometimes when he mentions she &amp; it is unclear of who is the she until you read it several times &amp; finally figure this out. I read that James was critical of this work yet critics find it is faultless and I think this 771 page book could have been done in 400 pages. I am glad I read this book but it took me awhile to get into the groove where it flowed nicely did not happen until about 3/4 into the story. The first chapter was the hardest for me because James uses metaphors &amp; his attempts to make it more interesting had me lost many times. This chapter introduces Kate Croy &amp; her family which are not main characters &amp; rarely mentioned in the storyline later. There are many unanswered questions which were never clear to the reader but not important to the main story. The ending is perfect &amp; was worth the effort to finish this, yet it has you guessing &amp; wondering. During this story I kept trying to imagine where James would take his characters &amp; see if they changed from their inherent personality but they stayed true to themselves. This is about a love triangle but more so two ends of the triangle then the other. Kate Croy (British), Milly Theale (wealthy American) &amp; Merton Densher (British). Kate &amp; Merton are poor and in love which is kept secret due to her Aunt Maud, who likes only Kate and does not care for Kate's family which include her widowed sister &amp; scandalized father. Aunt Maud does not approve of Kate marrying unless approved by her &amp; someone with money or position. Kate &amp; Merton have an intense uncommon romance which is more cerebral. Americans Milly Theale &amp; her traveling partner Susan Stringham, middle age friend whose more a mother to this orphaned young wealthy young lady, meet Aunt Maud &amp; Kate in London. Milly is not certain if she is terminally ill &amp; seeks a physician for answers. She meets Merton Densher who she first met in New York City. She is in love with Merton but her pride prevents her from proclaiming &amp; wonders about Kate's feelings towards Merton. Kate sees Milly's feelings for him &amp; encourages him to be nice to Milly. Duplicity becomes clear &amp; her money is the desire for the pair but all Kate's plan and Merton following for the love of Kate. Is the grass greener on the other side? This questions with regards to being poor &amp; thinking wealth is everything or being wealthy but wanting something you can't buy. Deceiving others &amp; self interest is a common theme throughout and a perfect ending.</span>
  <a data-text-id="706224708014069897" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_646623753" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/646623753" />
    <a title="Captain Sir Roddy, R.N. (Ret.)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2482609-captain-sir-roddy-r-n-ret"><img alt="Captain Sir Roddy, R.N. (Ret.)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1453308913p2/2482609.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/646623753?book_show_action=true">Jun 20, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Captain Sir Roddy, R.N. (Ret.)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Captain Sir Roddy, R.N. (Ret.)" href="/user/show/2482609-captain-sir-roddy-r-n-ret">Captain Sir Roddy, R.N. (Ret.)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780679455127" href="/book/show/924503.The_Wings_of_the_Dove">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2482609-captain-sir-roddy-r-n-ret?shelf=american-literature">american-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2482609-captain-sir-roddy-r-n-ret?shelf=author-henry-james">author-henry-james</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2482609-captain-sir-roddy-r-n-ret?shelf=my-western-canon">my-western-canon</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2482609-captain-sir-roddy-r-n-ret?shelf=everymans-library-editions">everymans-library-editions</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2482609-captain-sir-roddy-r-n-ret?shelf=read-in-2013">read-in-2013</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2482609-captain-sir-roddy-r-n-ret?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2482609-captain-sir-roddy-r-n-ret?shelf=victorian-literature">victorian-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2482609-captain-sir-roddy-r-n-ret?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2482609-captain-sir-roddy-r-n-ret?shelf=2014-favorites">2014-favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2482609-captain-sir-roddy-r-n-ret?shelf=american-authors-translators">american-authors-translators</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer646623753" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7645224311550512118">July 2014--This is my second time reading <i>The Wings of the Dove</i> in less than a year. It is a truly stunning novel and is becoming a another one of James's novels that I think is near perfection, like that of <i>The Portrait of a Lady</i>. I am finding on this second careful and thoughtful re-read that I am actually experiencing the novel rather than simply reading it. <br/><br/>Kate Croy and Merton Densher are 'a pair to draw to' for sure. As much as I love Kate the woman--fiercely independent, stubborn, intelli</span>
  <span id="freeText7645224311550512118" style="display:none">July 2014--This is my second time reading <i>The Wings of the Dove</i> in less than a year. It is a truly stunning novel and is becoming a another one of James's novels that I think is near perfection, like that of <i>The Portrait of a Lady</i>. I am finding on this second careful and thoughtful re-read that I am actually experiencing the novel rather than simply reading it. <br /><br />Kate Croy and Merton Densher are 'a pair to draw to' for sure. As much as I love Kate the woman--fiercely independent, stubborn, intelligent, and oh so clever--she's a top predator, a lioness stalking her prey, and there's almost a tinge of the sociopath to her. I dare not elaborate too much more for fear of giving away much of the plot, but suffice it to say that I think honor and integrity do not weigh as high in her values toolbox. Now, in defense of Kate Croy, I'll grant you that she has had a tough life; a mother who died early; a father who is completely spent and is considered a pariah by his social peers; a sister with children who is poor and reliant upon family for help; she lives with a domineering and opinionated aunt that endeavors to keep her on a tight leash; and, finally, she is in love with a man--Merton Densher--who has no money of his own. So, you do have to hand it to her that she is enterprising enough to take her own life (and Merton's as well) into her own hands. She is bound and determined to make a change--a change for the better.<br /><br />Merton Densher--the poor journalist--is the counterweight on the other end of the see-saw. His immediate problem is that Kate Croy 'wears the pants' in this family. She spins him like a top on the table. Having said that though, we do see through the course of the novel that Merton Densher does develop a spine, and slowly, but surely, his honor and integrity between to glow and burn like a white heat.<br /><br />The third leg of this 'stool' that is <i>The Wings of the Dove</i> is the incredibly rich--very, very rich--young American heiress, Miss Milly Theale. She met Merton while he was in the United States on assignment for his paper, and now she meets Kate Croy and Kate's aunt, Maud Lowder, and Merton again, of course, while in London on a European excursion. Well, Kate now sees an opportunity--maybe there is a way to obtain her family's consent to marry Merton, and just maybe that way is through Milly Theale. Oh, but a minor problem might exist--Milly is more than just a bit 'sweet' on Merton.<br /><br />Okay, I have sketched out the barest of details associated with this plot, and now I commend Henry James's brilliant novel into your capable hands. This is a psychological <i>tour de force</i> that will at various and several times leave you reeling in horror, shedding tears of great sadness, or nodding your head sagely as the tale is told page after page. But this story grabs you--heart and soul--and doesn't let up until the last sentence is read and digested; and it may well be one of the finest last sentences of all of modern fiction. <br /><br />The late Henry James may be viewed as impenetrable and too much work by some, but so is an artichoke or even a pomegranate, but what a reward and treat if you spend the time to carefully read and delve into the minds of his characters. If you're honest with yourself you'll realize that there are people you know and love right now that could step into (or out of) the pages of this novel. Reading <i>The Wings of the Dove</i> opens another incredibly unique window into what makes us singularly human. Sometimes it is ugly and sometimes it is beautiful, and Henry James has given us both in this splendid novel.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7645224311550512118" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer13330216626019820416">As usual, when it comes to a James novel, this one is filled with introspection, questions with unclear answers, assumptions, impressions, etc. Being perhaps, personally, the densest James novel I've read so far. I don't think it's my favorite (that would be The Ambassadors or The Golden Bowl) but it's still a great novel and one of my favorite books overall as I loved its pre-modernist tone; the characters' construction and development which was anything but plain; the way James blows the drama</span>
  <span id="freeText13330216626019820416" style="display:none">As usual, when it comes to a James novel, this one is filled with introspection, questions with unclear answers, assumptions, impressions, etc. Being perhaps, personally, the densest James novel I've read so far. I don't think it's my favorite (that would be The Ambassadors or The Golden Bowl) but it's still a great novel and one of my favorite books overall as I loved its pre-modernist tone; the characters' construction and development which was anything but plain; the way James blows the drama straight onto the reader from the very first page; the way the book lacks an orthodox closure plot-wise; and so much more artifices that make a James novel Jamesian.</span>

<div id="review_3115610064" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Leslie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11351200-leslie"><img alt="Leslie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1391045942p2/11351200.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audiobook 9781781980491" href="/book/show/44321277-the-wings-of-the-dove">review of another edition</a>

This book, for example, had all the makings of a great story but it bored me when it didn't anger me. I thought up several possible ending for the story only to find that the actual conclusion was dull and predictable. I have heard James praised for his female characters but, to me, they were all objectionable in one way o</span>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('MacbhgE7RFC3HStESNl8tqBPZhAlgG5FIjgfOOcLiQgFOELx+14pu6fVHtMbAS0VJICoKdvYG5GN1dfS/P4U+A==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('FDGNGUC5sys5SuydHR1kRUo7jqPcuwij9Z6aqspnZf4grtRuutzewCmC2QpOxTXmzvRAmiLjfXdac1JA0ZL4Dg==')})
  }
//]]>

H. Lawrence\'s greatest novel, Women in Love is both a lucid account of English society before the First World War, and a brilliant evocation of the inexorable power of human desire. \n\nWomen in Love continues where The Rainbow left off, with the third generation of Brangwens: Ursula Brangwen, now a teacher at Beldover, a mining town in the Midlands, and her sister Gudrun, who has returned from art school in London. The focus of the novel is primarily on their relationships, Ursula\'s with Rupert Birkin, a school inspector, and Gudrun\'s with industrialist Gerald Crich, and later with a sculptor, Loerke. Quintessentially modernist, Women in Love is one of Lawrence\'s most extraordinary, innovative and unsettling works.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"15329652386401134015\" href=\"#\" Eclipsed by a new breed of developers, financiers, and manufacturers, this pampered scion begins his gradual descent from the midwestern aristocracy to the working class. Today The Magnificent Ambersons is best known through the 1942 Orson Welles movie, but as the critic Stanley Kauffmann noted, &quot;It is high time that [the novel] appear again, to stand outside the force of Welles\'s genius, confident in its own right.&quot; &quot;The Magnificent Ambersons is perhaps Tarkington\'s best novel,&quot; judged Van Wyck Brooks. &quot;[It is] a typical story of an American family and town--the great family that locally ruled the roost and vanished virtually in a day as the town spread and darkened into a city.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/159.Henry_James">About Henry James</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/159.Henry_James"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1468309415p3/159.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/159.Henry_James">Henry James
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
2,636
followers
</div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer15647412551583902379">Henry James, OM, son of theologian Henry James Sr., brother of the philosopher and psychologist William James and diarist Alice James, was an American-born author, one of the founders and leaders of a school of realism in fiction. He spent much of his life in England and became a British subject shortly before his death. He is primarily known for a series of major novels in which he portrayed the </span>
  <span id="freeText15647412551583902379" style="display:none">Henry James, OM, son of theologian Henry James Sr., brother of the philosopher and psychologist William James and diarist Alice James, was an American-born author, one of the founders and leaders of a school of realism in fiction. He spent much of his life in England and became a British subject shortly before his death. He is primarily known for a series of major novels in which he portrayed the encounter of America with Europe. His plots centered on personal relationships, the proper exercise of power in such relationships, and other moral questions. His method of writing from the point of view of a character within a tale allowed him to explore the phenomena of consciousness and perception, and his style in later works has been compared to impressionist painting.<br />James insisted that writers in Great Britain and America should be allowed the greatest freedom possible in presenting their view of the world, as French authors were. His imaginative use of point of view, interior monologue and unreliable narrators in his own novels and tales brought a new depth and interest to realistic fiction, and foreshadowed the modernist work of the twentieth century. An extraordinarily productive writer, in addition to his voluminous works of fiction he published articles and books of travel writing, biography, autobiography, and criticism,and wrote plays, some of which were performed during his lifetime with moderate success. His theatrical work is thought to have profoundly influenced his later novels and tales.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15647412551583902379" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
